<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2013 4:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-10-18"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The kiwifruit (Actinidia chinensis) is an economically and nutritionally important fruit crop with remarkably high vitamin C content. Here we report the draft genome sequence of a heterozygous kiwifruit, assembled from ~140-fold next-generation sequencing data. The assembled genome has a total length of 616.1&#8201;Mb and contains 39,040 genes. Comparative genomic analysis reveals that the kiwifruit has undergone an ancient hexaploidization event (&#947;) shared by core eudicots and two more recent whole-genome duplication events. Both recent duplication events occurred after the divergence of kiwifruit from tomato and potato and have contributed to the neofunctionalization of genes involved in regulating important kiwifruit characteristics, such as fruit vitamin C, flavonoid and carotenoid metabolism. As the first sequenced species in the Ericales, the kiwifruit genome sequence provides a valuable resource not only for biological discovery and crop improvement but also for evolutionary and comparative genomics analysis, particularly in the asterid lineage. The kiwifruit is an economically and nutritionally important fruit crop with high vitamin C content. Here, the authors report the draft genome sequence of a heterozygous kiwifruit and through comparative genomic analysis provide valuable insight into kiwifruit evolution.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

                    <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Draft genome of the kiwifruit <i>Actinidia chinensis</i></h1>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The kiwifruit (<i>Actinidia chinensis</i>) is an economically and nutritionally important fruit crop with remarkably high vitamin C content. Here we report the draft genome sequence of a heterozygous kiwifruit, assembled from ~140-fold next-generation sequencing data. The assembled genome has a total length of 616.1âMb and contains 39,040 genes. Comparative genomic analysis reveals that the kiwifruit has undergone an ancient hexaploidization event (Î³) shared by core eudicots and two more recent whole-genome duplication events. Both recent duplication events occurred after the divergence of kiwifruit from tomato and potato and have contributed to the neofunctionalization of genes involved in regulating important kiwifruit characteristics, such as fruit vitamin C, flavonoid and carotenoid metabolism. As the first sequenced species in the Ericales, the kiwifruit genome sequence provides a valuable resource not only for biological discovery and crop improvement but also for evolutionary and comparative genomics analysis, particularly in the asterid lineage.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Actinidiaceae, the basal family within the Ericales, consists of the genera <i>Actinidia</i>, <i>Saurauia</i> and <i>Clematoclethra</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Dickison, W. C., Nowicke, J. W. &amp; Skvarla, J. J. Pollen morphology of the Dilleniaceae and Actinidiaceae. Am. J. Bot. 69, 1055â1073 (1982)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1074">1</a></sup>. The genus <i>Actinidia</i>, commonly known as kiwifruit, includes several economically important horticultural species, such as <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> Planchon, <i>A. deliciosa</i> (<i>A. chinensis</i> var. <i>deliciosa</i> A. Chevalier), <i>A. arguta</i> (Siebold and Zuccarini) Planchon ex Miquel and <i>A. eriantha</i> Bentham<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chat, J., Jauregui, B., Petit, R. J. &amp; Nadot, S. Reticulate evolution in kiwifruit (Actinidia, Actinidiaceae) identified by comparing their maternal and paternal phylogenies. Am. J. Bot. 91, 736â747 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1100">2</a></sup>. Approximately 54 species and 75 taxa have been described in <i>Actinidia</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Li, J. Q., Li, X. W. &amp; Soejarto, D. D. Actinidiaceae. inFlora of China eds Wu Z. Y., Raven P. H., Hong D. Y.) Vol 12, 334â362Science Press; Missouri Plant Garden Press (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1107">3</a></sup>, all of which are perennial, deciduous and dioecious plants with a climbing or straggling growth habit. The kiwifruit species are often reticulate polyploids with a base chromosome number of <i>x</i>=29 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="McNeilage, M. A. &amp; Considine, J. A. Chromosome studies in some Actinidia taxa and implications for breeding. N. Z. J. Bot. 27, 71â81 (1989)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1113">4</a>).</p><p>The kiwifruit has long been called âthe king of fruitsâ because of its remarkably high vitamin C content and balanced nutritional composition of minerals, dietary fibre and other health-beneficial metabolites. Extensive studies on the metabolic accumulation of vitamin C, carotenoids and flavonoids have been reported in kiwifruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Ampomah-Dwamena, C. et al. The kiwifruit lycopene beta-cyclase plays a significant role in carotenoid accumulation in fruit. J. Exp. Bot. 60, 3765â3779 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1120">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Laing, W. A. et al. A highly specific L-galactose-1-phosphate phosphatase on the path to ascorbate biosynthesis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 16976â16981 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1123">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Laing, W. A., Wright, M. A., Cooney, J. &amp; Bulley, S. M. The missing step of the L-galactose pathway of ascorbate biosynthesis in plants, an L-galactose guanyltransferase, increases leaf ascorbate content. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 9534â9539 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1126">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ledger, S. E., Janssen, B. J., Karunairetnam, S., Wang, T. &amp; Snowden, K. C. Modified CAROTENOID CLEAVAGE DIOXYGENASE8 expression correlates with altered branching in kiwifruit (Actinidia chinensis). New Phytol. 188, 803â813 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1129">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Montefiori, M. et al. Identification and characterisation of F3GT1 and F3GGT1, two glycosyltransferases responsible for anthocyanin biosynthesis in red-fleshed kiwifruit (Actinidia chinensis). Plant J. 65, 106â118 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1132">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Park, Y. S. et al. Nutritional and pharmaceutical properties of bioactive compounds in organic and conventional growing kiwifruit. Plant Foods Hum. Nutr. 68, 57â64 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1135">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Pilkington, S. M., Montefiori, M., Jameson, P. E. &amp; Allan, A. C. The control of chlorophyll levels in maturing kiwifruit. Planta 236, 1615â1628 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1139">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Torabinejad, J., Donahue, J. L., Gunesekera, B. N., Allen-Daniels, M. J. &amp; Gillaspy, G. E. VTC4 is a bifunctional enzyme that affects myoinositol and ascorbate biosynthesis in plants. Plant Physiol. 150, 951â961 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1142">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zuo, L. L., Wang, Z. Y., Fan, Z. L., Tian, S. Q. &amp; Liu, J. R. Evaluation of antioxidant and antiproliferative properties of three Actinidia (Actinidia kolomikta, Actinidia arguta, Actinidia chinensis) extracts in vitro. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 13, 5506â5518 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1145">13</a></sup>. The centre of origin of kiwifruit is in the mountains and ranges of southwestern China. The kiwifruit has a short history of domestication, starting in the early 20th century when its seeds were introduced into New Zealand<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ferguson, A. R. Kiwifruit: a botanical review. Hortic. Rev. 1â64 (1984)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1149">14</a></sup>. Through decades of domestication and substantial efforts for selection from wild kiwifruits, numerous varieties have been developed and kiwifruits have become an important fresh fruit worldwide with an annual production of 1.44 million tons in 2011 ( <a href="http://faostat.fao.org">http://faostat.fao.org</a>).</p><p>Despite the availability of an extensive expressed sequenced tag (EST) database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Crowhurst, R. N. et al. Analysis of expressed sequence tags from Actinidia: applications of a cross species EST database for gene discovery in the areas of flavor, health, color and ripening. BMC Genomics 9, 351 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1163">15</a></sup> and several genetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Testolin, R. et al. A kiwifruit (Actinidia spp.) linkage map based on microsatellites and integrated with AFLP markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 103, 30 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1167">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Fraser, L. G. et al. A gene-rich linkage map in the dioecious species Actinidia chinensis (kiwifruit) reveals putative X/Y sex-determining chromosomes. BMC Genomics 10, 102 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1170">17</a></sup>, whole-genome sequence resources for the kiwifruit, which are critical for its breeding and improvement, are very limited. Kiwifruit belongs to the order Ericales in the asterid lineage. Currently, no genomes have been sequenced for species in the Ericales and in the asterid lineage only the genomes of Solanaceae species in the order Euasterids I, including the tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1174">18</a></sup> and potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1178">19</a></sup>, have been sequenced.</p><p>Here we sequence and analyse the genome of a heterozygous kiwifruit, âHongyangâ (<i>A. chinensis</i>), which is widely grown in China. The availability of this genome sequence not only provides insight into the underlying molecular basis of specific agronomically important traits of kiwifruit and its wild relatives but also presents a valuable resource for elucidating evolutionary processes in the asterid lineage.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>One female individual of a Chinese kiwifruit cultivar âHongyangâ was selected for whole-genome sequencing. âHongyangâ is a heterozygous diploid (2<i>n</i>=2<i>x</i>=58) that is derived from clonally selected wild germplasm in central China and has not been subjected to further selection and breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhen, Y., Li, Z., Huang, H. &amp; Wang, Y. Molecular characterization of kiwifruit (Actinidia) cultivars and selections using SSR markers. J. Am. Soc. Hort. Sci. 129, 374â382 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1206">20</a></sup>. Its oval shaped fruit has a hairy, greenish-brown skin, a slight green or golden outer pericarp and a red-flesh inner pericarp with rows of tiny, black, edible seeds. Its fruit is highly nutritional containing abundant levels of ascorbic acid (vitamin C), carotenoids, flavonoids and anthocyanins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S1</a>).</p><p>A total of 105.8âGb high-quality sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S2</a>) were generated using the Illumina HiSeq 2000 system. This represented approximately a 140 Ã coverage of the kiwifruit genome with an estimated size of 758âMb based on the flow cytometry analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Hopping, M. E. Flow cytometric analysis of Actinidia species. N. Z. J. Bot. 32, 85â93 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1219">21</a></sup>. <i>De novo</i> assembly of these sequences employing Allpaths-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513â1518 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1226">22</a></sup> yielded a draft genome of 616.1âMb, representing 81.3% of the kiwifruit genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The genome assembly consists of 21,713 contigs and 5,110 scaffolds (&gt;2âkb), with N50 sizes of 58.8 and 646.8âkb for contigs and scaffolds, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#Tab1">Table 1</a>). To determine scaffold placement on kiwifruit pseudochromosomes, a high-density genetic map was constructed using an F1 population derived from the cross between âHongyang-MS-01â (male) and <i>A. eriantha</i> âJiangshanjiaoâ (female). Genotyping of each individual in the F1 population was determined using SLAF-seq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sun, X. et al. SLAF-seq: An efficient method of large-scale de novo SNP discovery and genotyping using high-throughput sequencing. PLoS One 8, e58700 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1240">23</a></sup>. The final map spanned 5,504.5âcM across 29 linkage groups and was composed of 4,301 single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers, with a mean marker density of 1.28âcM per marker. Using 3,379 markers that were uniquely aligned to the assembled scaffolds, a total of 853 scaffolds were anchored to the 29 kiwifruit pseudochromosomes, comprising 73.4% (452.4âMb) of the kiwifruit genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Of the 853 anchored scaffolds, 491 could be oriented (333.6âMb, 73.7% of the anchored sequences).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Kiwifruit genome assembly statistics.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Anchoring the Hongyang genome assembly to the diploid kiwifruit reference genetic map."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Anchoring the Hongyang genome assembly to the diploid kiwifruit reference genetic map.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3640/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3640/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3640_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3640/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3640_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="770"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>âHongyangâ (<i>A. chinensis</i>) genome scaffolds (blue) were anchored to the linkage groups (yellow) of the <i>A. chinensis</i> Ã <i>A. eriantha</i> genetic map with 3,379 SNP markers.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3640/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The GC content of the assembled genome was 35.2%, similar to that of the genomes of tomato (34%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1603">18</a></sup> and potato (34.8%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1607">19</a></sup>, which to date are the evolutionarily closest species of kiwifruit that have genomes sequenced (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>). Furthermore, we detected heterozygous sites by mapping the reads back to the assembled genome, revealing a high level of heterozygosity (0.536%) in âHongyangâ, which was further supported by the K-mer distribution of the genomic reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Fig. S2</a>).</p><p>To evaluate the quality of the assembled genome, an independent Illumina library with an insert size of 500âbp was constructed and sequenced. The resulting reads were mapped to the assembled genome to identify homozygous SNPs and structure variations (SVs), which represent potential base errors and misassemblies in the genome, respectively. The analyses indicated that the assembly has a single base error rate of 0.03%, which is comparable to the rate of the tomato genome (0.02%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1620">18</a></sup>. In addition, only 24 SVs were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S3</a>), indicating a very low frequency of misassemblies in the genome. The quality of the assembly was further assessed by aligning the EST sequences from the genus <i>Actinidia</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Crowhurst, R. N. et al. Analysis of expressed sequence tags from Actinidia: applications of a cross species EST database for gene discovery in the areas of flavor, health, color and ripening. BMC Genomics 9, 351 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1629">15</a></sup> to the assembled genome. The analysis indicated that the assembly contained 97.3% of the 81,956 ESTs derived from <i>A. chinensis</i>, 90.9% of the 83,924 ESTs from <i>A. deliciosa</i> and 94.3% of the 19,574 ESTs from <i>A. eriantha</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S4</a>). Together, these analyses supported the high quality of our genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repetitive sequence annotation</h3><p>We identified a total of ~222âMb (36% of the assembly) of repetitive sequences in the kiwifruit genome. The content of repetitive sequences in the kiwifruit genome appears to be much less than that in tomato (63.2%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1654">18</a></sup> and potato (62.2%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1658">19</a></sup>, whereas it is more than that in Arabidopsis (14%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1662">24</a></sup> and <i>Thellungiella parvula</i> (7.5%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913â918 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1669">25</a></sup>.</p><p>Comparative analysis with known repeats in Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jurka, J. Repeats in genomic DNA: mining and meaning. Curr. Opin. Struct. Biol. 8, 333â337 (1998)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1676">26</a></sup> and plant repeat database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ouyang, S. &amp; Buell, C. R. The TIGR Plant Repeat Databases: a collective resource for the identification of repetitive sequences in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D360âD363 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1680">27</a></sup> indicated that 68.8% of the repetitive sequences in the kiwifruit genome could be classified and annotated. A large portion of the unclassified repetitive sequences might be kiwifruit-specific. Retrotransposons made up the majority of the repeats, among which the long terminal repeat (LTR) family was the most abundant (~13.4% of the assembly). Within the LTR family, Copia and Gypsy represented the two most abundant subfamilies. In addition, DNA transposons accounted for ~4.75% of the genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Using the EST sequences of <i>A. chinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Crowhurst, R. N. et al. Analysis of expressed sequence tags from Actinidia: applications of a cross species EST database for gene discovery in the areas of flavor, health, color and ripening. BMC Genomics 9, 351 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1697">15</a></sup> and RNA-seq data we have generated from <i>A. chinensis</i> leaf and fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Fig. S3</a>), integrated with <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions and homologous sequence searching, we predicted a total of 39,040 protein-encoding genes with an average coding sequence length of 1,073âbp and 4.6 exons per gene. Among these genes, 74.5 and 82.3% had significant similarities to sequences in the non-redundant nucleotide and protein databases in NCBI, respectively. Additionally, 37.4, 66.9, 81.9, 61.3 and 81.8% could be annotated using COG, GO, TrEMBL, Swissprot and KEGG databases, respectively. Furthermore, conserved domains in &gt;65.5% of the predicted protein sequences could be identified by comparing them against InterPro and Pfam databases. In addition, a total of 2,438 putative transcription factors that are distributed in 58 families and 447 transcriptional regulators distributed in 22 families were identified in the kiwifruit genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Data 1 and 2</a>). In addition to protein-coding genes, 293 rRNAs, 511 tRNAs, 236 miRNAs, 91 snRNAs and 307 SnoRNAs were also identified.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparative analyses between kiwifruit and other plants</h3><p>Comparative analyses of the complete gene sets of kiwifruit, Arabidopsis, rice, grape and tomato were performed. A total of 25,381 genes in the kiwifruit genome were assigned into 13,100 orthologous gene clusters. Among these clusters, 7,985 are common to all five species, whereas 885 are confined to eudicots (kiwifruit, Arabidopsis, grape and tomato). Within the eudicots, 337 gene clusters are restricted to plants with flesh fruits (kiwifruit, grape and tomato), whereas 1,455 clusters contain genes only from kiwifruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Further functional characterization based on GO terms revealed that the 337 flesh fruit-specific families were highly enriched with genes associated with fruit quality, including those related to flavonoid, phenylpropanoid, anthocyanin and oligosaccharide metabolism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S6</a>). The kiwifruit-specific families were significantly enriched with genes related to pollen tube reception and specification of floral organ identity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S7</a>), both of which are consistent with the high diversity of sex expression found in kiwifruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Fraser, L. G. et al. A gene-rich linkage map in the dioecious species Actinidia chinensis (kiwifruit) reveals putative X/Y sex-determining chromosomes. BMC Genomics 10, 102 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1730">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Seal, A. G., Ferguson, A. R., De Silva, H. N. &amp; Zhang, J. L. The effect of 2n gametes on sex ratios in Actinidia. Sex. Plant. Reprod. 25, 197â203 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1733">28</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Venn diagram of orthologous gene families."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Venn diagram of orthologous gene families.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3640/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp"
