src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3640/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3640_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="595"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Five species (kiwifruit, Arabidopsis, grape, tomato and rice) were used to generate the Venn diagram based on the gene family cluster analysis.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3640/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Among plants with the sequenced genomes, tomato has the closest evolutionary relationship to kiwifruit. Consequently, the largest number of gene clusters (10,849) were shared between kiwifruit and tomato, representing 82.8 and 82.5% of their individual total gene clusters, respectively. We then calculated the evolutionary rate for each of the orthologous gene pairs of kiwifruitâgrape, Arabidopsisâgrape and tomatoâgrape. The average ratio (<i>Ï</i>) of non-synonymous (Ka) versus synonymous (Ks) nucleotide substitution rate in kiwifruit (0.064) was found to be greater than that in Arabidopsis (0.055) and tomato (0.052), indicating that diversifying selection may have been stronger in kiwifruit.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Whole-genome duplication in kiwifruit</h3><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) followed by gene loss has been found in most eudicots and is regarded as the major evolutionary force that gives rise to gene neofunctionalization in both plants and animals. Within the kiwifruit genome, 588 paralogous relationships were identified, covering 46% of the genome. We then compared the kiwifruit genome sequence to that of tomato, potato and grape, respectively, and identified a large number of syntenic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). The distribution of 4DTv (transversions at fourfold degenerate sites) and Ks values of homologous pairs in these syntenic regions, as well as the mean Ks values of individual syntenic blocks indicated that an ancient WGD (the Î³ event), which is shared by core eudicots, and two recent WGD events had occurred in the evolutionary history of kiwifruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Fig. S4aâc</a>). In addition, using the method described in Simillion <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Simillion, C., Vandepoele, K., Van Montagu, M. C., Zabeau, M. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. The hidden duplication past of Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 99, 13627â13632 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1783">29</a></sup>, we were able to group kiwifruit syntenic blocks into three age classes based on their mean Ks values, further supporting the ancient triplication and the two recent WGD events in kiwifruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Fig. S4d</a>). The two recent WGD events, <i>Ad-</i>Î± and <i>Ad-</i>Î², were estimated to have occurred ~26.7 and 72.9â101.4 million years ago, respectively, based on Ks of paralogous genes. These results are consistent with previous findings based on the EST analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Shi, T., Huang, H. &amp; Barker, M. S. Ancient genome duplications during the evolution of kiwifruit (Actinidia) and related Ericales. Ann. Bot. 106, 497â504 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1797">30</a></sup>. Both <i>Ad-</i>Î± and <i>Ad-</i>Î² events occurred after the kiwifruitâtomato or kiwifruitâpotato divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Comparative analysis and duplication events in the kiwifruit genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Comparative analysis and duplication events in the kiwifruit genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3640/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3640/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3640_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3640/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3640_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="405"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Syntenic blocks between genomes of kiwifruit, tomato and grape. (<b>b</b>) Whole-genome duplications in kiwifruit as revealed by the distribution of 4DTv distance between syntenically orthologous genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3640/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The relationship of orthologous genes in syntenic blocks between kiwifruit and grape was further analysed. We found that 55.8% of kiwifruit gene models are in blocks that are orthologous to one grape region, collectively covering 73.6% of the grape gene space. Among these grape genomic regions, 19.1% have one orthologous region in kiwifruit, 20.5% have two, 23% have three, 19.3% have four, 11% have five, 4.4% have six, 2.2% have seven and a few (&lt;1%) have eight, nine and ten. This pattern is similar to that of Arabidopsis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944â1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1840">31</a></sup>, whose genome has also undergone two WGD (At-Î± and At-Î²) following the ancient Î³ triplication. These data further supported the occurrence of the two recent WGD events in kiwifruit, followed by extensive gene loss.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene expansion and neofunctionalization in kiwifruit</h3><p>Kiwifruit is well known for its high nutritional value because of the extremely abundant content of ascorbic acid (vitamin C) in them. We investigated and compared genes involved in the ascorbic acid biosynthesis and recycling pathway in kiwifruit, Arabidopsis, grape, sweet orange and tomato. Although we found no expansion in genes from the L-galactose pathway that forms the major route to vitamin C biosynthesis in kiwifruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Bulley, S. M. et al. Gene expression studies in kiwifruit and gene over-expression in Arabidopsis indicates that GDP-L-galactose guanyltransferase is a major control point of vitamin C biosynthesis. J. Exp. Bot. 60, 765â778 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1852">32</a></sup>, we did find that other gene families involved in ascorbic acid biosynthesis, including Alase (aldonolactonase), APX (L-ascorbate peroxidase) and MIOX (myo-inositol oxygenase), and genes responsible for ascorbic acid regeneration from its oxidized forms, including MDHAR (monohydroascorbate reductase), exhibited an expansion in kiwifruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>). Phylogenetic analyses of genes in these expanded families, combined with results from the synteny analyses, indicated that the two recent WGDs in kiwifruit resulted in additional gene family members that evolved to contribute to the high vitamin C accumulation in the fruit of kiwifruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#Fig4">Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Figs S6âS8</a>). Most of the expanded genes in the ascorbic acid biosynthesis and recycling pathway were expressed in both leaves and fruits of kiwifruit, with a large portion being expressed higher in fruits (especially immature fruits) than in leaves (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S9</a>). This is consistent with the high level of ascorbic acid in both fruits and leaves of âHongyangâ and a higher level in immature fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Phylogenetic and syntenic analyses of monohydroascorbate reductase genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Phylogenetic and syntenic analyses of monohydroascorbate reductase genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3640/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3640/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3640_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3640/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3640_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="756"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Phylogenetic tree of monohydroascorbate reductase (MDHAR) genes from kiwifruit (green), Arabidopsis (blue), grape (purple), sweet orange (yellow) and tomato (red). Heatmaps representing expression levels of kiwifruit MDHAR genes in leaf, immature fruit, mature green fruit and ripe fruit (from left to right) are shown on the right of the tree. Log2-transformed gene expression values were used to generate heatmaps. (<b>b</b>) Microsynteny of genome regions surrounding kiwifruit MDHAR genes (Achn297231 and Achn389481), and their corresponding tomato (Solyc02g086710), potato (PGSC0003DMG400000486) and grape (GSVIVT01032453001) orthologues. MDHAR genes are shown in red. Genes with no syntenic homologue(s) are in white. Other syntenic genes are shown in different colours based on their presence/absence patterns in the genome regions.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3640/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Kiwifruit also contains high levels of other important nutritional compounds including carotenoids, flavonoids and chlorophylls (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S1</a>). Compared with Arabidopsis, grape, sweet orange and tomato, expansions of gene families in the carotenoid biosynthesis pathway, including lycopene beta-cyclase, lycopene epsilon-cyclase, phytoene desaturase and violaxanthin deepoxidase, were observed in kiwifruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Fig. S9</a>). In the flavonoid biosynthesis pathway, gene families including chalcone isomerase, flavanone 3-hydroxylase and flavonoid 3â²-hydroxylase, were expanded (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Fig. S10</a>). Although no large-scale gene expansion events in chlorophyll biosynthesis and degradation are evident in kiwifruit, an additional member encoding GluTR (Glutamyl-tRNA reductase) was found (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S12</a>). GluTR is responsible for the biosynthesis of 5-aminolevulinic acid, the key precursor of chlorophyll<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Hedtke, B., Alawady, A., Chen, S., Bornke, F. &amp; Grimm, B. HEMA RNAi silencing reveals a control mechanism of ALA biosynthesis on Mg chelatase and Fe chelatase. Plant Mol. Biol. 64, 733â742 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1923">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Kumar, A. M., Schaub, U., SÃ¶ll, D. &amp; Ujwal, M. L. Glutamyl-transfer RNA: at the crossroad between chlorophyll and protein biosynthesis. Trends Plant Sci. 1, 371â376 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1926">34</a></sup>. Expression analyses indicated that almost all of the identified expanded genes in the carotenoid, flavonoid and chlorophyll metabolic pathways were expressed, exhibiting temporal and tissue specificity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Tables S13âS15</a>).</p><p>It is worth noting that unlike most other kiwifruit cultivars, âHongyangâ fruits and leaves are also highly abundant in anthocyanins, one class of flavonoid compounds and responsible for the red colour of the inner pericarp of âHongyangâ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S1</a>). It is not surprising that there is no expansion observed in kiwifruit for the key enzymes of anthocyanin biosynthesis including leucoanthocyanidin dioxygenase/anthocyanidin synthase and UDP-glucose flavonoid 3-O-glucosyltransferase (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S11</a>). Most genes in the leucoanthocyanidin dioxygenase/anthocyanidin synthase and UDP-glucose flavonoid 3-O-glucosyltransferase families were highly expressed in both immature fruits and leaves, indicating that anthocyanins might be mainly synthesized in the early development stage of fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Table S14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Disease-resistance genes in kiwifruit</h3><p>Plants have evolved two layers of innate immunity to defend potential pathogens: pathogen-associated molecular pattern-triggered immunity (PTI) and effector-triggered immunity. PTI is a relatively ancient form that is triggered through the perception of pathogen-associated molecular patterns by pattern-recognition receptors, whereas effector-triggered immunity is conferred through the recognition of pathogen-secreted effectors by the nucleotide-binding site and leucine-rich repeat (NBSâLRR) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Jones, J. D. &amp; Dangl, J. L. The plant immune system. Nature 444, 323â329 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1953">35</a></sup>. In the kiwifruit genome, a total of 96 NBSâLRR genes were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Data 3</a>), which was comparable to the number of NBSâLRR genes found in papaya (55)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991â996 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1960">36</a></sup> and watermelon (44)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Guo, S. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet. 45, 51â58 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1964">37</a></sup> but considerably fewer than those in Arabidopsis (166)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Meyers, B. C., Kozik, A., Griego, A., Kuang, H. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Genome-wide analysis of NBS-LRR-encoding genes in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 15, 809â834 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1968">38</a></sup>, rice (~600)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Goff, S. A. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science 296, 92â100 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1973">39</a></sup>, grape (504)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1977">40</a></sup> and tomato (251)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1981">18</a></sup>. As particular NBSâLRR genes recognize specific pathogen effectors, the fewer number of NBSâLRR genes in kiwifruit may represent less potential for pathogen recognition. These data imply that NBSâLRR genes are not under strong selection pressure in kiwifruit, possibly because of fewer pathogens that have evolved to adapt to kiwifruit. The distribution of NBSâLRR genes in the kiwifruit genome is not random, as nearly one-third of them (27) are located in the genome within 10 clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Data 3</a>), suggesting that they have evolved mainly through tandem duplications, similar to what have been reported in other sequenced plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Guo, S. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet. 45, 51â58 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1988">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Meyers, B. C., Kozik, A., Griego, A., Kuang, H. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Genome-wide analysis of NBS-LRR-encoding genes in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 15, 809â834 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1991">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Goff, S. A. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science 296, 92â100 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1994">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d39657e1997">40</a></sup>.</p><p>A total of 261 putative pattern-recognition receptor genes, which encode receptor-like kinases with an LRR domain (RLKâLRR), were identified in the kiwifruit genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3640#MOESM526">Supplementary Data 4</a>). This number is larger than that found in Arabidopsis (220), grape (232) and tomato (236), suggesting that PTI, a type of ancient innate immunity, is more conserved in kiwifruit and may have an important role in defense against potential pathogens.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>A high-quality draft genome of a highly heterozygous kiwifruit cultivar âHongyangâ has been successfully assembled, using the high coverage (~140 Ã ) of Illumina paired-end and mate-pair reads. The assembly covers about 81.3% of the kiwifruit genome. The kiwifruit genome sequence presented in this study represents the first genome sequence of a member in the order Ericales and the third in the entire asterid lineage, after potato and tomato, thus providing a valuable resource for comparative genomics and evolutionary studies, especially in the asterid lineage that has much less available genomic resources compared with the rosid lineage.</p><p>Besides the ancient hexaploidization event (Î³) shared by core eudicots, kiwifruit appears to have undergone two additional independent WGD events. Both events occurred after the divergence of kiwifruit from tomato and potato. Whether these two WGD events are shared by other members of Actinidiaceae or the Ericales will require further analysis when more genome sequences from the family or order become available.</p><p>Kiwifruit is a rich source of ascorbic acid (vitamin C) and other health-beneficial compounds. Our analyses demonstrated that extensive expansions have occurred in the members of gene families involved in the ascorbic acid biosynthetic and recycling pathway, the carotenoid biosynthesis pathway and the flavonoid metabolism pathway. The majority of these expansions can be attributed to at least one of the two recent WGD events, indicating that WGD has played an important role in adding new gene family members that mediate important fruit-specific attributes that contribute to the high nutritional value of kiwifruit.</p><p>The kiwifruit genome sequence will be an invaluable resource for the genetic improvement of kiwifruit and for better understanding of genome evolution. It will also be invaluable in developing new varieties and for resolving questions of agronomical and/or biological importance, such as those related to fruit development and ripening, fruit nutrient metabolism, disease resistance, sex determination and polyploidy evolution in kiwifruit and other related plant species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>High-quality genomic DNA was extracted from young leaves of a 5-year old, female plant of <i>A. chinensis</i> cv. Hongyang, growing in the farm of Sichuan Academy of Natural Resource Sciences, Sichuan Province, China. An improved CTAB method was used to prepare the kiwifruit genomic DNA. The modified CTAB extraction buffer included 0.1âM Tris-HCl, 0.02âM EDTA, 1.4âM NaCl, 3% (w/v) CTAB and 5% (w/v) PVP K40. Beta-mercaptoethanol was added to the CTAB extraction buffer to ensure DNA integrity and quality. RNase A and proteinase K were used to remove RNA and protein contamination, respectively. Paired-end and mate-pair Illumina genomic DNA libraries were constructed following the manufacturerâs instructions (Illumina, USA) using the prepared DNA from âHongyangâ. The libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 system. Raw reads were processed by removing PCR duplicates, low-quality reads, adaptor sequences and contaminated reads of bacterial or viral origin. Additionally, sequence errors were corrected based on the K-mer frequency. The resulting high-quality cleaned reads were assembled into contigs and scaffolds using Allpaths-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513â1518 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2040">22</a></sup>, based on the âde bruijn graphâ theory. Gaps within the scaffolds were filled using the GapCloser tool in the SOAP<i>denovo</i> package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2047">41</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genetic map construction and scaffold anchoring</h3><p>An F1 population was generated from a cross between <i>A. chinensis</i> âHongyang-MS-01â (male) and <i>A. eriantha</i> âJiangshanjiaoâ (female). âHongyang-MS-01â is a male variety and is normally used as the pollinizer for the female cultivar âHongyangâ. Owing to the high heterozygosity in both parents, the F1 population could be considered as a double pseudo-testcross. A total of 300 individuals were obtained and 108 of them were used for genetic map construction.</p><p>High-quality genomic DNA was extracted from the 108 individuals and was used to construct Illumina sequencing libraries following the manufacturerâs protocol (Illumina, USA). The genomic libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 system, and a total of 3.76âGb of 80âbp reads were obtained. Average sequencing depths of sequenced loci per parent and per progeny were 78.63- and 10.73-fold, respectively. Genotyping and evaluation of the quality of genetic markers were performed as described in Sun <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sun, X. et al. SLAF-seq: An efficient method of large-scale de novo SNP discovery and genotyping using high-throughput sequencing. PLoS One 8, e58700 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2071">23</a></sup> Briefly, the Illumina paired-end reads were first clustered into the same groups if they share at least 90% sequence identities. Alleles at each locus were then defined using the minimum allele frequency evaluation, and markers with more than four alleles were discarded. The accuracy of the genotyping was evaluated based on the coverage of each allele and the number of single-nucleotide polymorphisms at each locus. A total of 5,320 high-quality SNP markers were identified and used to construct the genetic map with the double pseudo-testcross strategy using JoinMap 4.0. Using the nearest-neighbour method, a total of 4,301 markers were clustered into 29 linkage groups, with LOD scores ranging from 4 to 20. The SNP markers were then aligned to the kiwifruit-assembled scaffolds, and only uniquely aligned markers were used to anchor and orient the scaffolds onto the 29 kiwifruit pseudochromosomes. The marker sequences are provided in Supplementary Data 5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Quality assessment and heterozygous locus identification</h3><p>An independent library with an insert size of 500âbp was constructed and sequenced to assess the quality of the genome assembly. Paired-end reads from this library were aligned to the kiwifruit genome assembly using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2083">42</a></sup>. On the basis of the alignments, genotypes supported by at least 10 reads and with an allele frequency <span class="stix">â©¾</span>0.3 were assigned to each genomic position. Homozygous SNPs, which represent potential sequence errors in the assembly, were then identified. SVs, which represent potential misassemblies, were also identified using SVDetect<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Zeitouni, B. et al. SVDetect: a tool to identify genomic structural variations from paired-end and mate-pair sequencing data. Bioinformatics 26, 1895â1896 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2087">43</a></sup>. To determine the coverage of gene space by the assembly, EST sequences of <i>A. chinensis</i>, <i>A. deliciosa</i> and <i>A. eriantha</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Crowhurst, R. N. et al. Analysis of expressed sequence tags from Actinidia: applications of a cross species EST database for gene discovery in the areas of flavor, health, color and ripening. BMC Genomics 9, 351 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2099">15</a></sup> were mapped to the genome assembly using BLASTN.</p><p>To identify heterozygous sites, reads from all four short-insert (insert size <span class="stix">â©½</span>500âbp) Illumina paired-end libraries were aligned to the kiwifruit genome sequences using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2106">42</a></sup>. Only read pairs that were uniquely aligned to the genome were kept. Following alignment, the coverage of each genomic position by bases A, G, C and T was calculated. Genomic loci containing at least two alleles with each of them supported by at least 20 reads and with allele frequency of at least 0.3 were identified as heterozygous loci in the kiwifruit genome. Adjacent heterozygous loci that were separated by &lt;5âbp were discarded.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Identification of repetitive sequence</h3><p>Repeat sequences in the kiwifruit genome were first identified using three <i>de novo</i> prediction programs, LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2121">44</a></sup>, RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, (Suppl 1): i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2125">45</a></sup> and PILER-DF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myers, E. W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21, (Suppl 1): i152âi158 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2129">46</a></sup>. The identified repeat sequences were used to construct a non-redundant repeat sequence library. Repeat sequences in the kiwifruit genome were then identified using Repeatmasker ( <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) and the constructed repeat sequence library. Additional repeat sequences were identified by comparing the assembled genome sequences against the Repbase database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jurka, J. Repeats in genomic DNA: mining and meaning. Curr. Opin. Struct. Biol. 8, 333â337 (1998)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2141">26</a></sup> and the plant repeat database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ouyang, S. &amp; Buell, C. R. The TIGR Plant Repeat Databases: a collective resource for the identification of repetitive sequences in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D360âD363 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2145">27</a></sup>, using BLAST with an <i>e</i>-value cutoff of 1e-5. Finally, repeat sequences with an identity <span class="stix">â©¾</span>50% were grouped into the same classes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">RNA-seq data generation and expression analysis</h3><p>Mature leaves, immature fruits (20 days after pollination (DAP)), mature green fruits (120 DAP) and ripe fruits (127 DAP) were collected from a 5-year-old âHongyangâ plant. Fresh tissues were immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen and ground to fine powder. Total RNAs were isolated using the Trizol reagent (Invitrogen, USA) followed by treatment with RNase-free DNase I (Promega, USA) according to the manufacturersâ protocols. The quality of RNAs was checked using an Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer. Illumina RNA-Seq libraries were prepared and sequenced on a HiSeq 2000 system following the manufacturerâs instructions (Illumina, USA). Two to three biological replicates were performed for the fruit samples. The resulting paired-end 90-bp- or single-end 50-bp RNA-seq reads were first aligned to ribosomal RNA and tRNA sequences in order to remove possible contaminations of these sequences. The cleaned reads were then aligned to the kiwifruit genome assembly using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics. 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2161">47</a></sup>. Following the alignment, raw counts for each kiwifruit gene model were derived and normalized to fragments per kilobase of exon model per million mapped reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2165">48</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>The repeat-masked kiwifruit genome sequences were used for gene prediction. The gene prediction pipeline combined <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions, homologous sequence searching and transcriptome sequence mapping (including both ESTs and RNA-seq data). The results from the three independent methods were merged into the final consensus of gene models using Glean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Elsik, C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2180">49</a></sup>. Specifically, GeneScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78â94 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2184">50</a></sup> was employed for <i>de novo</i> gene prediction. Homologous sequence searching was performed by comparing protein sequences of Arabidopsis, rice, grape and tomato against the repeat-masked kiwifruit genome sequences using TBLASTN with parameters of identity <span class="stix">â©¾</span>60 and <span class="stix">â©¾</span>80% of the query sequence covered in the alignments. The corresponding kiwifruit genomic regions were retrieved, together with sequences 1âkb downstream and upstream of the aligned regions. The alignments were further processed using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2191">51</a></sup> to extract accurate exonâintron information. Illumina RNA-seq reads were assembled <i>de novo</i> into contigs using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2199">48</a></sup>. The resulting contig sequences, as well as <i>A. chinensis</i> EST sequences, were aligned to the repeat-masked kiwifruit genome sequences using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT--the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2206">52</a></sup>. Final gene sequences were derived through further analysis of the BLAT alignment results using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654â5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2210">53</a></sup>.</p><p>Annotation of the predicted genes was performed by blasting their sequences against a number of nucleotide and protein sequence databases, including COG, InterPro, nt, nr, KEGG, Swiss-Prot and TrEMBL, using an <i>e-</i>value cutoff of 1e-5. Functions of the predicted kiwifruit genes were assigned using AHRD (Automated assignment of Human Readable Descriptions; <a href="https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD">https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD</a>) as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2227">18</a></sup>. Briefly, the 200 top-scoring search results of kiwifruit-predicted proteins against Swiss-Prot, TrEMBL, InterPro and Arabidopsis protein databases were scored based on alignment scores, expected quality of descriptions per database and a lexical scoring of individual âwordsâ computed from their frequency in the descriptions of top-scoring results. The highest-scoring description was assigned to each kiwifruit gene. GO terms were assigned to the annotated genes in kiwifruit and other sequenced plant species, including tomato, grape, apple and strawberry, using the Blast2GO pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674â3676 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2231">54</a></sup>. tRNAs were identified using tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2235">55</a></sup>, and snRNAs and snoRNAs were identified by searching the genome assembly against the Rfam database using INFERNAL with default parameters ( <a href="http://infernal.janelia.org/">http://infernal.janelia.org/</a>). rRNAs were identified by searching the genome assembly using the Rfam database as a reference with a cutoff of at least 90% sequence identity and 80% coverage. Transcription factors/regulators were identified and classified into different families using the iTAK pipeline ( <a href="http://bioinfo.bti.cornell.edu/tool/itak">http://bioinfo.bti.cornell.edu/tool/itak</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Comparative analysis of gene sets</h3><p>Protein sequences from kiwifruit, Arabidopsis, rice, grape and tomato were used to identify gene clusters. For those having spliced variants, only the variants with the longest protein sequences were used. Gene family clusters among different plant species were identified using OrthoMCL 1.4 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2261">56</a>). Pairwise sequence similarities between all protein sequences were calculated using BLASTP with an <i>e-</i>value cutoff of 1e-05. On the basis of the results of BLASTP, OrthoMCL was used to perform a Markov clustering algorithm to define the cluster structure, with a default inflation value (âI) of 1.5.</p><p>KaKs_Calculator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Zhang, Z. et al. KaKs_Calculator: calculating Ka and Ks through model selection and model averaging. Genomics Proteomics Bioinformatics 4, 259â263 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2271">57</a></sup> was utilized to calculate the value under the evolution press. The phylogenetic trees were constructed using MEGA 5.0 using the neighbour-joining or maximum likelihood method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2275">58</a></sup>. The parameters used in the tree construction were the JTT model plus gamma-distributed rates and 1,000 bootstraps.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Comparative genomics analysis</h3><p>All-to-all BLASTP analysis of protein sequences was performed between kiwifruit and grape, tomato, and potato, respectively, as well as within each species, using an <i>e</i>-value cutoff of 1e-10, coverage <span class="stix">â©¾</span>50% and identity <span class="stix">â©¾</span>20%. Syntenic regions within each species and between kiwifruit and grape, tomato and potato were then identified using MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944â1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2290">31</a></sup> based on the all-to-all BLASTP results. Protein sequences of homologous gene pairs in the identified syntenic regions were first aligned using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2294">59</a></sup>, and the protein alignments were then converted to the CDS alignments. Finally, 4DTV values were calculated on these CDS alignments and corrected using the HKY model, and Ks values were calculated using the Yn00 program in the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2298">60</a></sup>. The kiwifruit syntenic blocks were grouped into different age classes based on their mean Ks values using the method described in Simillion <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Simillion, C., Vandepoele, K., Van Montagu, M. C., Zabeau, M. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. The hidden duplication past of Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 99, 13627â13632 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2306">29</a></sup> Briefly, two duplication blocks were put into the same age class if the mean Ks values of both duplications did not differ significantly using a <i>t</i>-test (<i>P</i>&lt;0.01). A candidate age class was formed by taking a first duplication and adding to it the duplication that resulted in the age class with the lowest coefficient of variance. This process continued until no further duplications could be added to the age class without exceeding a coefficient of variance value of 0.5. Next, a second candidate age class was formed by starting with a second duplication and repeating the process. These steps were repeated for the remaining duplications until no more age classes could be defined containing five or more blocks.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Nutrient metabolite determinations</h3><p>The content of various metabolites, including starch, sugar, chlorophyll, flavonoids, anthocyanins, carotenoids and ascorbic acid (vitamin C), was determined in âHongyangâ leaves and fruits (including both inner and outer pericarps) at 20 DAP, 120 DAP and 127 DAP. Three biological replicates were performed for each analysis. Starch content was measured using the starch iodine reaction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Fan, X., Mattheis, J. P., Patterson, M. E. &amp; Fellman, J. K. Changes in amylose and total starch content in âFujiâ apples during maturation. Hort. Sc. 30, 104â105 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2324">61</a></sup>. Briefly, samples extracted with 18% HCl were stained with the I<sub>2</sub>-KI solution. After mixing, absorbance at 605 and 530ânM was measured. The starch content was estimated according to the standard curve generated by the two standards of amylose and amylopectin mixed in various ratios. Sugar content was determined using the anthrone method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Gaudy, A. F. Colorimetric determination of protein and carbohydrate. Ind. Water Wastes 7, 17â22 (1962)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2330">62</a></sup>, with glucose as the standard. Flavonoids were extracted in 95% ethanol by ultrasonic methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Karabegovic, I. T., Veljkovic, V. B. &amp; Lazic, M. L. Ultrasound-assisted extraction of total phenols and flavonoids from dry tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum) leaves. Nat. Prod. Commun. 6, 1855â1856 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2334">63</a></sup>. The level of flavonoids was measured using the aluminium chloride colorimetric method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Chang, C., Yang, M., Wen, H. &amp; Chern, J. Estimation of total flavonoid content in propolis by two complementary colorimetric methods. J. Food Drug Anal. 10, 178â182 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2338">64</a></sup>, with rutin as the standard. Total anthocyanin content was determined by the method described in Di Stefano <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Di Stefano, R., Cravero, M. C. &amp; Gentilini, N. Metodi per lo studio dei polifenoli dei vini. LâEnotecnico I. Maggio 25, 83â89 (1989)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2346">65</a></sup> Briefly, 1-g frozen sample was macerated twice to colourless with 25âml extracting solvent (95% ethanol-0.lâN HCl) at 50âÂ°C. Ten millilitre of filtered extract was diluted to 50âml with pH=1.0 (0.2âmolâl<sup>â1</sup> KCL-0.2âmolâl<sup>â1</sup> HCl-H2O) and pH=4.5 (19.294âg NaAc and 24âml glacial acetic acid make up to 500âml with ultrapure water) buffer solution, respectively. The diluted extract was stored in the dark for 100âmin, and the optical density (OD) was measured at the absorption maxima of anthocyanins extract (530ânm). The anthocyanin content was calculated as <i>X</i>=(ÎOD Ã <i>V</i> Ã <i>F</i> Ã <i>M</i> Ã 1,000)/(<i>Îµ</i> Ã <i>m</i>). Where <i>X</i>=total anthocyanin content (mgâg<sup>â1</sup>); OD=absorbancy reading on the diluted sample (1âcm cell); <i>V</i>=diluted volume (ml); <i>F</i>=dilution factor; <i>M</i>=molecular weight of cyanidin-3-O-glucoside (449.38); <i>Îµ</i>=molar extinction coefficient of cyanidin-3-O-glucoside (2.69 Ã 104); and <i>m</i>=sample weight. The content of ascorbic acid (vitamin C) was measured using the dinitrophenylhydrazine method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Otles, S. Comparative determination of ascorbic acid in bass (Morone lebrax) liver by HPLC and DNPH methods. Int. J. Food Sci. Nutr. 46, 229â232 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2394">66</a></sup>. Chlorophyll and carotenoids were repeatedly extracted in 80% aqueous acetone (10âml) in darkness until the samples turned white. Absorbance was measured at 470, 663 and 645ânm, respectively. The relative content of chlorophyll a, chlorophyll b, total chlorophyll and total carotenoids was then calculated using formulae described in Lichtenthaler <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Lichtenthaler, H. K. &amp; Wellburn, A. R. Determinations of total carotenoids and chlorophylls a and b of leaf extracts in different solvents. Biochem. Soc. Trans. 11, 591â592 (1983)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2402">67</a></sup> and Arnon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Arnon, D. T. Copper enzymes in isolated chloroplasts polyphenoloxidase in Beta vulgaris. Plant Physiol. 24, 1â15 (1949)." href="/articles/ncomms3640#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d39657e2406">68</a></sup>: total carotenoids (mgâg<sup>â1</sup>)=(1,000 Ã A470â3.27 Ã chlorophyll aâ104 Ã chlorophyll b)/229, chlorophyll a (mgâg<sup>â1</sup>)=(12.7 Ã A663â2.69 Ã A645) Ã <i>V</i>/(1,000 Ã <i>W</i>), chlorophyll b (mgâg<sup>â1</sup>)=(22.9 Ã A645â4.68 Ã A663) Ã <i>V</i>/(1,000 Ã <i>W</i>), total chlorophyll (mgâg<sup>â1</sup>)=(8.02 Ã A663+20.20 Ã A645) Ã <i>V</i>/(1,000 Ã <i>W</i>), where <i>V</i>=volume of the extract (ml) and <i>W</i>=weight of fresh tissues (g).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec21" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec21-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec21">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec21-content"><p><b>URLs.</b> Kiwifruit Genome Database, <a href="http://bioinfo.bti.cornell.edu/kiwi">http://bioinfo.bti.cornell.edu/kiwi</a>; FAO Statistics database, <a href="http://faostat.fao.org">http://faostat.fao.org</a></p><p><b>Accession codes:</b> Sequence data have been deposited in GenBank/EMBL/DDBJ nucleotide core database under the accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AONS00000000">AONS00000000</a>. Draft genome of the kiwifruit <i>Actinidia chinensis</i>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 4:2640 doi: 10.1038/ncomms3640 (2013).</p></div></div></section>
